<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Social anxiety disorder</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>

<tr class="noprint">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
<div style="text-align: right;">[<a class="extiw" href="https://www.wikidata.org/wiki/Q281928" title="d:Q281928">edit on Wikidata</a>]</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Social anxiety disorder</b> (<b>SAD</b>), also known as <b>social phobia</b>, is an <a href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorder" title="Anxiety disorder">anxiety disorder</a> characterized by a significant amount of fear in one or more social situations causing considerable distress and impaired ability to function in at least some parts of daily life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2013_1-0"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:15</sup> These fears can be triggered by perceived or actual scrutiny from others.</p>
<p>Physical symptoms often include excessive <a href="/wiki/Blushing" title="Blushing">blushing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyperhidrosis" title="Hyperhidrosis">excess sweating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tremor" title="Tremor">trembling</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palpitations" title="Palpitations">palpitations</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stutter" title="Stutter">Stammering</a> may be present, along with rapid speech. <a href="/wiki/Panic_attack" title="Panic attack">Panic attacks</a> can also occur under intense fear and discomfort. Some sufferers may use <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> or other <a href="/wiki/Psychoactive_drug" title="Psychoactive drug">drugs</a> to reduce fears and inhibitions at social events. It is common for sufferers of social phobia to <a href="/wiki/Self-medication" title="Self-medication">self-medicate</a> in this fashion, especially if they are undiagnosed, untreated, or both; this can lead to <a href="/wiki/Alcoholism" title="Alcoholism">alcoholism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eating_disorders" title="Eating disorders">eating disorders</a> or other kinds of substance abuse. SAD is sometimes referred to as an "illness of lost opportunities" where "individuals make major life choices to accommodate their illness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SteinM2001_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-SteinM2001-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Supplement_to_Health_Reports_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Supplement_to_Health_Reports-3">[3]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/ICD-10" title="ICD-10">ICD-10</a> guidelines, the main diagnostic criteria of social anxiety disorder are fear of being the focus of attention, or fear of behaving in a way that will be embarrassing or humiliating, avoidance and anxiety symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-4">[4]</a></sup> Standardized rating scales can be used to screen for social anxiety disorder and measure the severity of anxiety.</p>
<p>The first line treatment for social anxiety disorder is <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">cognitive behavioral therapy</a> (CBT) with medications recommended only in those who are not interested in therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilling2013_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pilling2013-5">[5]</a></sup> CBT is effective in treating social phobia, whether delivered individually or in a group setting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> The cognitive and behavioral components seek to change thought patterns and physical reactions to anxiety-inducing situations. The attention given to social anxiety disorder has significantly increased since 1999 with the approval and marketing of drugs for its treatment. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prescription_drugs" title="Prescription drugs">Prescribed medications</a> include several classes of <a href="/wiki/Antidepressant" title="Antidepressant">antidepressants</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitors" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors">selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors</a> (SSRIs), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serotonin-norepinephrine_reuptake_inhibitor" title="Serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitor">serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitors</a> (SNRIs), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase_inhibitors" title="Monoamine oxidase inhibitors">monoamine oxidase inhibitors</a> (MAOIs).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Other commonly used medications include <a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blockers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a>. It is the most common anxiety disorder with up to 10% of people being affected at some point in their life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lancet2016_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lancet2016-8">[8]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cognitive_aspects">Cognitive aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Cognitive aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_model" title="Cognitive model">cognitive models</a> of social anxiety disorder, those with social phobias experience <a href="/wiki/Angst" title="Angst">dread</a> over how they will be presented to others. They may feel overly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Self-conscious" title="Self-conscious">self-conscious</a>, pay high self-attention after the activity, or have high performance standards for themselves. According to the <a href="/wiki/Social_psychology" title="Social psychology">social psychology</a> theory of <a href="/wiki/Impression_management" title="Impression management">self-presentation</a>, a sufferer attempts to create a well-mannered impression towards others but believes he or she is unable to do so. Many times, prior to the potentially anxiety-provoking social situation, sufferers may deliberately review what could go wrong and how to deal with each unexpected case. After the event, they may have the <a href="/wiki/Perception" title="Perception">perception</a> that they performed unsatisfactorily. Consequently, they will perceive anything that may have possibly been abnormal as embarrassing. These thoughts may extend for weeks or longer. <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_distortion" title="Cognitive distortion">Cognitive distortions</a> are a hallmark, and are learned about in CBT (cognitive-behavioral therapy). Thoughts are often self-defeating and inaccurate. Those with social phobia tend to interpret neutral or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambiguous" title="Ambiguous">ambiguous</a> conversations with a negative outlook, and many studies suggest that socially anxious individuals remember more negative memories than those less distressed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furmark_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-furmark-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>An example of an instance may be that of an employee presenting to his co-workers. During the presentation, the person may stutter a word, upon which he or she may worry that other people significantly noticed and think that their perceptions of him or her as a presenter have been tarnished. This cognitive thought propels further anxiety which compounds with further stuttering, sweating, and, potentially, a panic attack.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Behavioral_aspects">Behavioral aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Behavioral aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Social anxiety disorder is a persistent fear of one or more situations in which the person is exposed to possible scrutiny by others and fears that he or she may do something or act in a way that will be humiliating or embarrassing. It exceeds normal "shyness" as it leads to excessive social avoidance and substantial social or occupational impairment. Feared activities may include almost any type of social interaction, especially small groups, <a href="/wiki/Dating" title="Dating">dating</a>, parties, talking to strangers, restaurants, interviews, etc.</p>
<p>Those who suffer from social anxiety disorder fear being judged by others in society. In particular, individuals with social anxiety are nervous in the presence of people with authority and feel uncomfortable during physical examinations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneier_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schneier-10">[10]</a></sup> People who suffer from this disorder may behave a certain way or say something and then feel embarrassed or humiliated after. As a result, they oftentimes choose to isolate themselves from society to avoid such situations. They may also feel uncomfortable meeting people they do not know, and act distant when they are with large groups of people. In some cases, they may show evidence of this disorder by avoiding eye contact, or blushing when someone is talking to them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneier_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schneier-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to psychologist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B.F._Skinner" title="B.F. Skinner">B.F. Skinner</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phobias" title="Phobias">phobias</a> are controlled by <a href="/wiki/Escape_response" title="Escape response">escape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avoidance_response" title="Avoidance response">avoidance behaviors</a>. For instance, a student may leave the room when talking in front of the class (escape) and refrain from doing verbal presentations because of the previously encountered anxiety attack (avoid). Major avoidance behaviors could include an almost pathological/compulsive lying behavior in order to preserve self-image and avoid judgement in front of others. Minor avoidance behaviors are exposed when a person avoids <a href="/wiki/Eye_contact" title="Eye contact">eye contact</a> and crosses his/her arms to avoid recognizable <a href="/wiki/Tremor" title="Tremor">shaking</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furmark_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-furmark-9">[9]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Fight-or-flight_response" title="Fight-or-flight response">fight-or-flight response</a> is then triggered in such events.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiological_aspects">Physiological aspects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Physiological aspects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Physiological effects, similar to those in other anxiety disorders, are present in social phobics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-physiology_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-physiology-12">[12]</a></sup> In adults, it may be <a href="/wiki/Tears" title="Tears">tears</a> as well as excessive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sweat" title="Sweat">sweating</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Difficulty_breathing" title="Difficulty breathing">difficulty breathing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tremor" title="Tremor">shaking</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palpitation" title="Palpitation">palpitations</a> as a result of the fight-or-flight response. The walk disturbance (where a person is so worried about how they walk that they may lose balance) may appear, especially when passing a group of people. <a href="/wiki/Blushing" title="Blushing">Blushing</a> is commonly exhibited by individuals suffering from social phobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furmark_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-furmark-9">[9]</a></sup> These visible symptoms further reinforce the anxiety in the presence of others. A 2006 study found that the area of the <a href="/wiki/Brain" title="Brain">brain</a> called the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Limbic_system" title="Limbic system">limbic system</a>, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperactive" title="Hyperactive">hyperactive</a> when patients are shown threatening faces or confronted with frightening situations. They found that patients with more severe social phobia showed a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Correlation" title="Correlation">correlation</a> with the increased response in the amygdala.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comorbidity">Comorbidity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Comorbidity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>SAD shows a high degree of <a href="/wiki/Comorbidity" title="Comorbidity">comorbidity</a> (co-occurrence) with other psychiatric disorders. In fact, a population-based study found that 66% of those with SAD had one or more additional mental health disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> SAD often occurs alongside low <a href="/wiki/Self-esteem" title="Self-esteem">self-esteem</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a>, perhaps due to a lack of personal relationships and long periods of isolation related to avoidance of social situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-15">[15]</a></sup> To try to reduce their anxiety and alleviate depression, people with social phobia may use alcohol or other drugs, which can lead to <a href="/wiki/Substance_abuse" title="Substance abuse">substance abuse</a>. It is estimated that one-fifth of patients with social anxiety disorder also suffer from <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_dependence" title="Alcohol dependence">alcohol dependence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> However, some research suggests SAD is unrelated to, or even protective against, alcohol-related problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> One of the most common complementary psychiatric conditions is depression, with clinical depression being 1.49 to 3.5 times more likely to occur in those with SAD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.64.8.903.7Cnoedit_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.64.8.903.7Cnoedit-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.58.3.251.7Cnoedit_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.58.3.251.7Cnoedit-20">[20]</a></sup> Aside from depression, other common disorders diagnosed in patients with SAD are other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorders" title="Anxiety disorders">anxiety disorders</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Generalized_anxiety_disorder" title="Generalized anxiety disorder">generalized anxiety disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Avoidant_personality_disorder" title="Avoidant personality disorder">Avoidant personality disorder</a> is also highly correlated with SAD, with comorbidity rates ranging from 25% to 89%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceB_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceB-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Patients who suffer from both alcoholism and social anxiety disorder are more likely to avoid group-based treatments and are more likely to relapse compared to people who do not have both disorders simultaneously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>The DSM-IV criteria stated that an individual cannot receive a diagnosis of social anxiety disorder if their symptoms are better accounted for by one of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autism_spectrum_disorders" title="Autism spectrum disorders">autism spectrum disorders</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asperger_syndrome" title="Asperger syndrome">Asperger syndrome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Joshi_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Joshi-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>Because of its close relationship and overlapping symptoms with other illnesses, treating people with social phobia may help understand underlying connection in other psychiatric disorders. There is research indicating that social anxiety disorder is often correlated with <a href="/wiki/Bipolar_disorder" title="Bipolar disorder">bipolar disorder</a> and <a href="/wiki/Attention_deficit_hyperactivity_disorder" title="Attention deficit hyperactivity disorder">attention deficit hyperactivity disorder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> Some researchers believe they share an underlying cyclothymic-anxious-sensitive disposition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> In addition, studies show that more socially phobic patients treated with anti-depressant medication develop <a href="/wiki/Hypomania" title="Hypomania">hypomania</a> than non-phobic controls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> The hypomania can be seen as the medication creating a new problem.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Causes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="/wiki/Research" title="Research">Research</a> into the causes of social anxiety and social phobia is wide-ranging, encompassing multiple perspectives from <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>. Scientists have yet to pinpoint the exact <a href="/wiki/Etiology" title="Etiology">causes</a>. Studies suggest that genetics can play a part in combination with environmental factors. Social phobia is not caused by other mental disorders or by substance abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> Generally, social anxiety begins at a specific point in an individual's life. This will develop over time as the person struggles to recover. Eventually, mild social awkwardness can develop into symptoms of social anxiety or phobia.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetics">Genetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Genetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>It has been shown that there is a two to threefold greater risk of having social phobia if a first-degree relative also has the disorder. This could be due to <a href="/wiki/Genetics" title="Genetics">genetics</a> and/or due to children acquiring social fears and avoidance through processes of <a href="/wiki/Observational_learning" title="Observational learning">observational learning</a> or parental <a href="/wiki/Psychosocial" title="Psychosocial">psychosocial</a> education. Studies of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Identical_twins" title="Identical twins">identical twins</a> brought up (via <a href="/wiki/Adoption" title="Adoption">adoption</a>) in different families have indicated that, if one twin developed social anxiety disorder, then the other was between 30 percent and 50 percent more likely than average to also develop the disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> To some extent this 'heritability' may not be specific – for example, studies have found that if a parent has any kind of anxiety disorder or clinical depression, then a child is somewhat more likely to develop an anxiety disorder or social phobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Studies suggest that parents of those with social anxiety disorder tend to be more <a href="/wiki/Solitude" title="Solitude">socially isolated</a> themselves (Bruch and Heimberg, 1994; Caster et al., 1999), and shyness in adoptive parents is significantly correlated with shyness in adopted children (Daniels and Plomin, 1985).</p>
<p>Growing up with overprotective and hypercritical parents has also been associated with social anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneier_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schneier-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Adolescents who were rated as having an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emotional_insecurity" title="Emotional insecurity">insecure</a> (anxious-ambivalent) attachment with their mother as infants were twice as likely to develop anxiety disorders by late adolescence,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> including social phobia.</p>
<p>A related line of research has investigated 'behavioural inhibition' in infants – early signs of an inhibited and introspective or fearful nature. Studies have shown that around 10–15 percent of individuals show this early temperament, which appears to be partly due to genetics. Some continue to show this trait into adolescence and adulthood, and appear to be more likely to develop social anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_experiences">Social experiences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Social experiences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A previous negative social experience can be a trigger to social phobia,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa-causes_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa-causes-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa-causes2_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa-causes2-38">[38]</a></sup> perhaps particularly for individuals high in '<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interpersonal" title="Interpersonal">interpersonal</a> sensitivity'. For around half of those diagnosed with social anxiety disorder, a specific <a href="/wiki/Psychological_trauma" title="Psychological trauma">traumatic</a> or humiliating social event appears to be associated with the onset or worsening of the disorder;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> this kind of event appears to be particularly related to <a href="/wiki/Specific_social_phobia" title="Specific social phobia">specific (performance) social phobia</a>, for example regarding public speaking (Stemberg <i>et al.</i>, 1995). As well as direct experiences, observing or hearing about the socially negative experiences of others (e.g. a faux pas committed by someone), or verbal warnings of social problems and dangers, may also make the development of a social anxiety disorder more likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Social anxiety disorder may be caused by the longer-term effects of not fitting in, or being <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bullied" title="Bullied">bullied</a>, rejected or ignored (Beidel and Turner, 1998). Shy adolescents or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Avoidant" title="Avoidant">avoidant</a> adults have emphasised unpleasant experiences with peers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> or childhood bullying or <a href="/wiki/Harassment" title="Harassment">harassment</a> (Gilmartin, 1987). In one study, popularity was found to be negatively correlated with social anxiety, and children who were neglected by their peers reported higher social anxiety and fear of negative evaluation than other categories of children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Socially phobic children appear less likely to receive positive reactions from peers<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and anxious or inhibited children may isolate themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_influences">Cultural influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Cultural influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cultural" title="Cultural">Cultural</a> factors that have been related to social anxiety disorder include a society's attitude towards shyness and avoidance, affecting the ability to form <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_relationship" title="Interpersonal relationship">relationships</a> or access employment or education, and <a href="/wiki/Shame" title="Shame">shame</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> One study found that the effects of parenting are different depending on the culture – American children appear more likely to develop social anxiety disorder if their parents emphasize the importance of others' opinions and use shame as a disciplinary strategy (Leung <i>et al.</i>, 1994), but this association was not found for Chinese/Chinese-American children. In <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, research has indicated that shy-inhibited children are more accepted than their <a href="/wiki/Peer_group" title="Peer group">peers</a> and more likely to be considered for leadership and considered competent, in contrast to the findings in Western countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> Purely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Demographic" title="Demographic">demographic</a> variables may also play a role.</p>
<p>Problems in developing social skills, or '<a href="/wiki/Social_fluency" title="Social fluency">social fluency</a>', may be a cause of some social anxiety disorder, through either inability or lack of <a href="/wiki/Confidence" title="Confidence">confidence</a> to interact socially and gain positive reactions and acceptance from others. The studies have been mixed, however, with some studies not finding significant problems in social skills<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> while others have.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> What does seem clear is that the socially anxious perceive their own social skills to be low.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> It may be that the increasing need for sophisticated social skills in forming relationships or careers, and an emphasis on assertiveness and competitiveness, is making social anxiety problems more common, at least among the '<a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">middle classes</a>'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> An interpersonal or media emphasis on 'normal' or 'attractive' personal characteristics has also been argued to fuel <a href="/wiki/Perfectionism_(psychology)" title="Perfectionism (psychology)">perfectionism</a> and feelings of inferiority or insecurity regarding negative evaluation from others. The need for social acceptance or social standing has been elaborated in other lines of research relating to social anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Substance_induced">Substance induced</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Substance induced">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>While <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> initially relieves social phobia, excessive alcohol misuse can worsen social phobia symptoms and can cause panic disorder to develop or worsen during alcohol intoxication and especially during <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Alcohol withdrawal syndrome">alcohol withdrawal syndrome</a>. This effect is not unique to alcohol but can also occur with long-term use of drugs which have a similar mechanism of action to alcohol such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepines" title="Benzodiazepines">benzodiazepines</a> which are sometimes prescribed as tranquillisers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Benzodiazepines possess anti-anxiety properties and can be useful for the short-term treatment of severe anxiety. Like the anticonvulsants, they tend to be mild and well tolerated, although there is a risk of habit-forming. Benzodiazepines are usually administered orally for the treatment of anxiety; however, occasionally lorazepam or diazepam may be given intravenously for the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Panic_attack" title="Panic attack">panic attacks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The World Council of Anxiety does not recommend benzodiazepines for the long-term treatment of anxiety due to a range of problems associated with long-term use including <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">tolerance</a>, psychomotor impairment, cognitive and memory impairments, <a href="/wiki/Physical_dependence" title="Physical dependence">physical dependence</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine_withdrawal_syndrome" title="Benzodiazepine withdrawal syndrome">benzodiazepine withdrawal syndrome</a> upon discontinuation of benzodiazepines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Despite increasing focus on the use of antidepressants and other agents for the treatment of anxiety, benzodiazepines have remained a mainstay of anxiolytic pharmacotherapy due to their robust efficacy, rapid onset of therapeutic effect, and generally favorable side effect profile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15762816_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid15762816-55">[55]</a></sup> Treatment patterns for psychotropic drugs appear to have remained stable over the past decade, with benzodiazepines being the most commonly used medication for panic disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid12900305_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid12900305-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>Approximately half of patients attending mental health services for conditions including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorders" title="Anxiety disorders">anxiety disorders</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Panic_disorder" title="Panic disorder">panic disorder</a> or social phobia are the result of <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> or <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine_dependence" title="Benzodiazepine dependence">benzodiazepine dependence</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Sometimes anxiety pre-existed <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">alcohol</a> or benzodiazepine dependence but the alcohol or benzodiazepine dependence act to keep the anxiety disorders going and often progressively making them worse.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Many people who are addicted to alcohol or prescribed benzodiazepines when it is explained to them they have a choice between ongoing ill mental health or quitting and recovering from their symptoms decide on quitting alcohol and/or their benzodiazepines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1995_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1995-57">[57]</a></sup> It was noted that every individual has an individual sensitivity level to alcohol or sedative hypnotic drugs and what one person can tolerate without ill health another will suffer very ill health and that even moderate drinking can cause rebound anxiety syndromes and sleep disorders.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> A person who is suffering the toxic effects of alcohol or benzodiazepines will not benefit from other therapies or medications as they do not address the root cause of the symptoms.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2009)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Symptoms may temporarily worsen however, during <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alcohol_withdrawal" title="Alcohol withdrawal">alcohol withdrawal</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine_withdrawal" title="Benzodiazepine withdrawal">benzodiazepine withdrawal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen1995_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen1995-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychological_factors">Psychological factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Psychological factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Research has indicated the role of 'core' or 'unconditional' negative beliefs (e.g. "I am inept") and 'conditional' beliefs nearer to the surface (e.g. "If I show myself, I will be rejected"). They are thought to develop based on personality and adverse experiences and to be activated when the person feels under threat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> One line of work has focused more specifically on the key role of self-presentational concerns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The resulting anxiety states are seen as interfering with social performance and the ability to concentrate on interaction, which in turn creates more social problems, which strengthens the negative <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Model_(abstract)" title="Model (abstract)">schema</a>. Also highlighted has been a high focus on and worry about anxiety symptoms themselves and how they might appear to others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> A similar model<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> emphasizes the development of a distorted mental representation of the self and overestimates of the likelihood and consequences of negative evaluation, and of the performance standards that others have. Such cognitive-behavioral models consider the role of negatively biased memories of the past and the processes of rumination after an event, and fearful <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anticipation_(emotion)" title="Anticipation (emotion)">anticipation</a> before it. Studies have also highlighted the role of subtle avoidance and defensive factors, and shown how attempts to avoid feared negative evaluations or use 'safety behaviors' (Clark &amp; Wells, 1995) can make social interaction more difficult and the anxiety worse in the long run. This work has been influential in the development of Cognitive Behavioral Therapy for social anxiety disorder, which has been shown to have efficacy.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanisms">Mechanisms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Mechanisms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are many studies investigating neural bases of social anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fears1_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-fears1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Although the exact neural mechanisms have not been found yet, there is evidence relating social anxiety disorder to imbalance in some neurochemicals and hyperactivity in some brain areas.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dopamine">Dopamine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Dopamine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sociability is closely tied to <a href="/wiki/Dopamine" title="Dopamine">dopamine</a> <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmission</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Misuse of stimulants like amphetamines to increase self-confidence and improve social performance is common.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In a recent study a direct relation between <span class="clarify-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">social status</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Clarify" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="This passage needs to be better explained. (December 2014)">clarify</span></a></i>]</sup> of volunteers and binding affinity of dopamine D2/3 receptors in the <a href="/wiki/Striatum" title="Striatum">striatum</a> was found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Other research shows that the binding affinity of dopamine D2 receptors in the striatum of social anxiety sufferers is lower than in controls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Some other research shows an abnormality in <a href="/wiki/Dopamine_transporter" title="Dopamine transporter">dopamine transporter</a> density in the striatum of social anxiety sufferers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sero_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-sero-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> However, some researchers have been unable to replicate previous findings of evidence of dopamine abnormality in social anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> Studies have shown high prevalence of social anxiety in Parkinson's disease and schizophrenia. In a recent study, social phobia was diagnosed in 50% of <a href="/wiki/Parkinson%27s_disease" title="Parkinson's disease">Parkinson's disease</a> patients.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Other researchers have found social phobia symptoms in patients treated with dopamine antagonists like haloperidol, emphasizing the role of dopamine neurotransmission in social anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-haloperidol_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-haloperidol-72">[72]</a></sup> <span class="citation-needed-content" style="padding-left:0.1em; padding-right:0.1em; color:#595959; border:1px solid #DDD;">Also, concentration problems, mental and physical fatigue, <a href="/wiki/Anhedonia" title="Anhedonia">anhedonia</a> and decreased self-confidence can be seen in those with social anxiety disorder.</span><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_neurotransmitters">Other neurotransmitters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Other neurotransmitters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some evidence points to the possibility that social anxiety disorder involves reduced <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> receptor binding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> A recent study reports increased <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> transporter binding in psychotropic medication-naive patients with generalized social anxiety disorder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sero_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-sero-68">[68]</a></sup> Although there is little evidence of abnormality in <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">serotonin</a> neurotransmission, the limited efficacy of medications which affect serotonin levels may indicate the role of this pathway. <a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">Paroxetine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sertraline" title="Sertraline">sertraline</a> are two SSRIs that have been confirmed by the FDA to treat social anxiety disorder. Some researchers believe that SSRIs decrease the activity of the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fears1_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-fears1-63">[63]</a></sup> There is also increasing focus on other candidate transmitters, e.g. norepinephrine and glutamate, which may be over-active in social anxiety disorder, and the inhibitory transmitter GABA, which may be under-active in the thalamus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fears1_63-2"><a href="#cite_note-fears1-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brain_areas">Brain areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Brain areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The amygdala is part of the <a href="/wiki/Limbic_system" title="Limbic system">limbic system</a> which is related to fear cognition and emotional learning. Individuals with social anxiety disorder have been found to have a hypersensitive <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a>; for example in relation to social threat cues (e.g. perceived negative evaluation by another person), angry or hostile faces, and while waiting to give a speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> Recent research has also indicated that another area of the brain, the <a href="/wiki/Anterior_cingulate_cortex" title="Anterior cingulate cortex">anterior cingulate cortex</a>, which was already known to be involved in the experience of physical pain, also appears to be involved in the experience of 'social pain', for example perceiving group exclusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> A 2007 meta-analysis also found that individuals with social anxiety had hyperactiviation in the <a href="/wiki/Amygdala" title="Amygdala">amygdala</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insular_cortex" title="Insular cortex">insula</a> areas which are frequently associated with fear and negative emotional processing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>ICD-10 defines social anxiety disorder (social phobias) as fear of scrutiny by other people leading to avoidance of social situations. The anxiety symptoms may present as a complaint of blushing, hand tremor, nausea or urgency of micturition. Symptoms may progress to panic attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-who.int-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<p>Standardized rating scales such as the <a href="/wiki/Social_Phobia_Inventory" title="Social Phobia Inventory">Social Phobia Inventory</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Social_Phobia_and_Anxiety_Inventory-Brief_form" title="Social Phobia and Anxiety Inventory-Brief form">SPAI-B</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liebowitz_Social_Anxiety_Scale" title="Liebowitz Social Anxiety Scale">Liebowitz Social Anxiety Scale</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Social_Interaction_Anxiety_Scale" title="Social Interaction Anxiety Scale">Social Interaction Anxiety Scale</a> can be used to screen for social anxiety disorder and measure the severity of anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Focus is increasing on the prevention of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anxiety_disorders" title="Anxiety disorders">anxiety disorders</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> Use of <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">CBT</a> and related techniques may decrease the number of children with social anxiety disorder following completion of prevention programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Psychotherapies">Psychotherapies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Psychotherapies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The first line treatment for social anxiety disorder is <a href="/wiki/Cognitive_behavioral_therapy" title="Cognitive behavioral therapy">cognitive behavioral therapy</a> with medications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitors" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors">selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors</a> (SSRIs) used only in those who are not interested in therapy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2013_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:191</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pilling2013_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Pilling2013-5">[5]</a></sup> Self-help based on principles of CBT is a second-line treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2013_1-2"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:191</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-British_Journal_of_Psychiatry_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-British_Journal_of_Psychiatry-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is some emerging evidence for the use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acceptance_and_Commitment_Therapy" title="Acceptance and Commitment Therapy">Acceptance and Commitment Therapy</a> (ACT) in the treatment of social anxiety disorder. ACT Is considered an offshoot of traditional CBT and emphasizes accepting unpleasant symptoms rather than fighting against them, as well as psychological flexibility - the ability to adapt to changing situational demands, to shift one's perspective, and to balance competing desires.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1016.2Fj.cpr.2010.03.001_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1016.2Fj.cpr.2010.03.001-88">[88]</a></sup> ACT may be useful as a second line treatment for this disorder in situations where CBT is ineffective or refused.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some studies have suggested social skills training (SST) can help with social anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Examples of social skills focused on during SST for social anxiety disorder include: initiating conversations, establishing friendships, interacting with members of the opposite sex, constructing a speech and assertiveness skills.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> However, it is not clear whether specific social skills techniques and training are required, rather than just support with general social functioning and exposure to social situations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></p>
<p>Given the evidence that social anxiety disorder may predict subsequent development of other psychiatric disorders such as depression, early diagnosis and treatment is important.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.64.8.903.7Cnoedit_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.64.8.903.7Cnoedit-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.58.3.251.7Cnoedit_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi10.1001.2Farchpsyc.58.3.251.7Cnoedit-20">[20]</a></sup> Social anxiety disorder remains under-recognized in <a href="/wiki/Primary_care" title="Primary care">primary care</a> practice, with patients often presenting for treatment only after the onset of <a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">complications</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression">clinical depression</a> or substance abuse disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p>A 2014 systematic review of interventions for adults with social anxiety disorder identified following psychotherapic interventions from published and unpublished sources. Outcomes were validated measures of social anxiety, reported as standardised mean differences compared with waitlist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Medications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="SSRIs">SSRIs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: SSRIs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Selective_serotonin_reuptake_inhibitor" title="Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitor">Selective serotonin reuptake inhibitors</a> (SSRIs), a class of antidepressants, are first choice medication for generalized social phobia but a second line treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICE2013_1-3"><a href="#cite_note-NICE2013-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:191</sup> Compared to older forms of medication, there is less risk of tolerability and drug dependency associated with SSRIs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 1995 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Double-blind" title="Double-blind">double-blind</a>, <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a>-controlled trial, the SSRI paroxetine was shown to result in clinically meaningful improvement in 55 percent of patients with generalized social anxiety disorder, compared with 23.9 percent of those taking placebo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssri-1995-study_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssri-1995-study-99">[99]</a></sup> An October 2004 study yielded similar results. Patients were treated with either <a href="/wiki/Fluoxetine" title="Fluoxetine">fluoxetine</a>, psychotherapy, or a placebo. The first four sets saw improvement in 50.8 to 54.2 percent of the patients. Of those assigned to receive only a placebo, 31.7 percent achieved a rating of 1 or 2 on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_Global_Impression-Improvement" title="Clinical Global Impression-Improvement">Clinical Global Impression-Improvement</a> scale. Those who sought both therapy and medication did not see a boost in improvement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssri-2004-study_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssri-2004-study-100">[100]</a></sup></p>
<p>General <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adverse_effect_(medicine)" title="Adverse effect (medicine)">side-effects</a> are common during the first weeks while the body adjusts to the drug. Symptoms may include <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headaches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nausea" title="Nausea">nausea</a>, <a href="/wiki/Insomnia" title="Insomnia">insomnia</a> and changes in sexual behavior. Treatment safety during pregnancy has not been established.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In late 2004 much media attention was given to a proposed link between SSRI use and suicidality [a term that encompasses suicidal ideation and attempts at suicide as well as suicide]. For this reason, [although evidential causality between SSRI use and actual suicide has not been demonstrated] the use of SSRIs in pediatric cases of depression is now recognized by the Food and Drug Administration as warranting a cautionary statement to the parents of children who may be prescribed SSRIs by a family doctor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-warning_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-warning-102">[102]</a></sup> Recent studies have shown no increase in rates of suicide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ssri-suicide_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-ssri-suicide-103">[103]</a></sup> These tests, however, represent those diagnosed with depression, not necessarily with social anxiety disorder.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_drugs">Other drugs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Other drugs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Other prescription drugs are also used, if other methods are not effective.</p>
<p>In 1985, before the introduction of SSRIs, anti-depressants such as <a href="/wiki/Monoamine_oxidase_inhibitor" title="Monoamine oxidase inhibitor">monoamine oxidase inhibitors</a> (MAOIs) were frequently used in the treatment of social anxiety. Their efficacy appears to be comparable or sometimes superior to SSRIs or benzodiazepines. However, because of the <a href="/wiki/Diet_(nutrition)" title="Diet (nutrition)">dietary</a> restrictions in the amino acid <a href="/wiki/Tyramine" title="Tyramine">tyramine</a> required, high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic" title="Toxic">toxicity</a> in overdose, and incompatibilities with other drugs, its usefulness as a treatment for social phobics is now limited. Some argue for their continued use, however, or that a special diet does not need to be strictly adhered to.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crozier-maoi_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-crozier-maoi-104">[104]</a></sup> A newer type of this medication, <a href="/wiki/Reversible_inhibitor_of_monoamine_oxidase_A" title="Reversible inhibitor of monoamine oxidase A">Reversible inhibitors of monoamine oxidase subtype A</a> (RIMAs) such as the drug <a href="/wiki/Moclobemide" title="Moclobemide">moclobemide</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17092192_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17092192-105">[105]</a></sup> bind reversibly to the MAO-A <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a>, greatly reducing the risk of <a href="/wiki/Hypertensive_crisis" title="Hypertensive crisis">hypertensive crisis</a> with dietary tyramine intake.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">Benzodiazepines</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Clonazepam" title="Clonazepam">clonazepam</a> are an alternative to SSRIs. These drugs are often used for short-term relief of severe, disabling anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> Although benzodiazepines are still sometimes prescribed for long-term everyday use in some countries, there is concern over the development of <a href="/wiki/Drug_tolerance" title="Drug tolerance">drug tolerance</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical_dependency" title="Chemical dependency">dependency</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drug_misuse" title="Drug misuse">misuse</a>. It has been recommended that benzodiazepines be considered only for individuals who fail to respond to other medications.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> Benzodiazepines augment the action of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABA" title="GABA">GABA</a>, the major inhibitory neurotransmitter in the brain; effects usually begin to appear within minutes or hours. In most patients, tolerance rapidly develops to the sedative effects of benzodiazepines, but not to the anxiolytic effects. Long-term use of benzodiazepine may result in physical dependence, and abrupt discontinuation of the drug should be avoided due to high potential for withdrawal symptoms (including tremor, insomnia, and in rare cases, seizures). A gradual tapering of the dose of clonazepam (a decrease of 0.25 mg every 2 weeks), however, has been shown to be well tolerated by patients with social anxiety disorder. Benzodiazepines are not recommended as monotherapy for patients who have major depression in addition to social anxiety disorder and should be avoided in patients with a history of substance abuse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schneier_10-3"><a href="#cite_note-Schneier-10">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Certain <a href="/wiki/Anticonvulsant" title="Anticonvulsant">anticonvulsant</a> drugs such as <a href="/wiki/Gabapentin" title="Gabapentin">gabapentin</a> are effective in social anxiety disorder and may be a possible treatment alternative to <a href="/wiki/Benzodiazepine" title="Benzodiazepine">benzodiazepines</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid17502773_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid17502773-108">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SchatzbergCole2010_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-SchatzbergCole2010-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serotonin-norepinephrine_reuptake_inhibitors" title="Serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitors">Serotonin-norepinephrine reuptake inhibitors</a> (SNRIs) such as <a href="/wiki/Venlafaxine" title="Venlafaxine">venlafaxine</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup> have shown similar effectiveness to the SSRIs. In Japan, <a href="/wiki/Milnacipran" title="Milnacipran">Milnacipran</a> is used in the treatment of <a href="/wiki/Taijin_kyofusho" title="Taijin kyofusho">Taijin kyofusho</a>, a Japanese variant of social anxiety disorder.</p>
<p>The novel antidepressant <a href="/wiki/Mirtazapine" title="Mirtazapine">mirtazapine</a> has been studied for the treatment of social anxiety disorder, and rendered mixed results.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Muelbacher_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Muelbacher-113">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Another atypical antidepressant, <a href="/wiki/Bupropion" title="Bupropion">bupropion</a>, has shown success in an open trial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bpsrsocial_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-bpsrsocial-115">[115]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some people with a form of social phobia called performance phobia have been helped by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-blocker" title="Beta-blocker">beta-blockers</a>, which are more commonly used to control high blood pressure. Taken in low doses, they control the physical manifestation of anxiety and can be taken before a public performance.</p>
<p>A novel treatment approach has recently been developed as a result of translational research. It has been shown that a combination of acute dosing of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D-cycloserine" title="D-cycloserine">d-cycloserine</a> (DCS) with exposure therapy facilitates the effects of exposure therapy of social phobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup> DCS is an old <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic" title="Antibiotic">antibiotic</a> medication used for treating tuberculosis and does not have any anxiolytic properties per se. However, it acts as an agonist at the glutamatergic N-methyl-D-aspartate (NMDA) receptor site, which is important for learning and memory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kava-kava" title="Kava-kava">Kava-kava</a> has also attracted attention as a possible treatment,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> although safety concerns exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lim_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lim-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sorrentino_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sorrentino-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="wikitable" style="float:right;">

<td align="left">Taiwan</td>
<td style="text-align:center;">7% child(2002~2008)
<p><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-A_study_of_the_developmental_trajectory_of_social_anxiety_among_boys_and_girls_from_4th_grade_to_11th_grade_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-A_study_of_the_developmental_trajectory_of_social_anxiety_among_boys_and_girls_from_4th_grade_to_11th_grade-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Social anxiety disorder is known to appear at an early age in most cases. Fifty percent of those who develop this disorder have developed it by the age of 11, and 80% have developed it by age 20.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> This early age of onset may lead to people with social anxiety disorder being particularly vulnerable to depressive illnesses, drug abuse and other psychological conflicts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SteinLancet2008_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-SteinLancet2008-133">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Prevalence" title="Prevalence">prevalence</a> estimates were based on the examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychiatric" title="Psychiatric">psychiatric</a> clinic samples, social anxiety disorder was thought to be a relatively rare disorder. The opposite was found to be true; social anxiety was common, but many were afraid to seek psychiatric help, leading to an underrecognition of the problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furmark_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-furmark-9">[9]</a></sup> Prevalence rates vary widely because of its vague diagnostic criteria and its overlapping symptoms with other disorders. There has been some debate on how the studies are conducted and whether the illness truly impairs the respondents as laid out in the official criteria. Psychologist <a href="/wiki/Ray_Crozier" title="Ray Crozier">Ray Crozier</a> argues, "it is difficult to ascertain whether the person being interviewed adheres to the DSM-III-R criteria or whether they are merely exhibiting poor <a href="/wiki/Social_skills" title="Social skills">social skills</a> or shyness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crozier-socialskills_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-crozier-socialskills-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/National_Comorbidity_Survey" title="National Comorbidity Survey">National Comorbidity Survey</a> of over 8,000 American correspondents in 1994 revealed 12-month and lifetime prevalence rates of 7.9 percent and 13.3 percent, respectively; this makes it the third most prevalent psychiatric disorder after depression and alcohol dependence, and the most common of the anxiety disorders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> According to U.S. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epidemiological" title="Epidemiological">epidemiological</a> data from the <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_of_Mental_Health" title="National Institute of Mental Health">National Institute of Mental Health</a>, social phobia affects 15 million adult Americans in any given year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> Cross-cultural studies have reached prevalence rates with the conservative rates at 5 percent of the population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SteinM2001_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-SteinM2001-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-crozier-prevalence_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-crozier-prevalence-137">[137]</a></sup> However, other estimates vary within 2 percent and 7 percent of the U.S. adult population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-surg-gen-prevalence_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-surg-gen-prevalence-138">[138]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_139-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-139">[139]</a></sup></p>
<p>The mean onset of social phobia is 10 to 13 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> Onset after age 25 is rare and is typically preceded by panic disorder or major depression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Social anxiety disorder occurs more often in females than males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> The prevalence of social phobia appears to be increasing among white, married, and well-educated individuals. As a group, those with generalized social phobia are less likely to graduate from high school and are more likely to rely on government financial assistance or have poverty-level salaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fda-demographics_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-fda-demographics-143">[143]</a></sup> Surveys carried out in 2002 show the youth of <a href="/wiki/England" title="England">England</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a> have a prevalence rate of 0.4 percent, 1.8 percent, and 0.6 percent, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-europe-prevalence_144-0"><a href="#cite_note-europe-prevalence-144">[144]</a></sup> In Canada, the prevalence of self-reported social anxiety for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nova_Scotian" title="Nova Scotian">Nova Scotians</a> older than 14 years was 4.2 percent in June 2004 with women (4.6 percent) reporting more than men (3.8 percent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa-nova-scotia_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa-nova-scotia-145">[145]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, social phobia is the 8th and 5th leading disease or illness for males and females between 15–24 years of age as of 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sa-australia_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-sa-australia-146">[146]</a></sup> Because of the difficulty in separating social phobia from poor social skills or shyness, some studies have a large range of prevalence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> The table also shows higher prevalence in Brazil.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Literary descriptions of shyness can be traced back to the days of <a href="/wiki/Hippocrates" title="Hippocrates">Hippocrates</a> around 400 B.C. Hippocrates described someone who "through bashfulness, suspicion, and timorousness, will not be seen abroad; loves darkness as life and cannot endure the light or to sit in lightsome places; his hat still in his eyes, he will neither see, nor be seen by his good will. He dare not come in company for fear he should be misused, disgraced, overshoot himself in gesture or speeches, or be sick; he thinks every man observes him."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first mention of the psychiatric term social phobia (<i>phobie des situations sociales</i>), was made in the early 1900s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Janet_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-Janet-149">[149]</a></sup> Psychologists used the term "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_neurosis" title="Social neurosis">social neurosis</a>" to describe extremely shy patients in the 1930s. After extensive work by <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Wolpe" title="Joseph Wolpe">Joseph Wolpe</a> on <a href="/wiki/Systematic_desensitization" title="Systematic desensitization">systematic desensitization</a>, research on phobias and their treatment grew. The idea that social phobia was a separate entity from other phobias came from the British psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Marks" title="Isaac Marks">Isaac Marks</a>, in the 1960s. This was accepted by the <a href="/wiki/American_Psychiatric_Association" title="American Psychiatric Association">American Psychiatric Association</a> and was first officially included in the third edition of the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders. The definition of the phobia was revised in 1989 to allow comorbidity with <a href="/wiki/Avoidant_personality_disorder" title="Avoidant personality disorder">avoidant personality disorder</a>, and introduced generalized social phobia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-furmark_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-furmark-9">[9]</a></sup> Social phobia had been largely ignored prior to 1985.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>After a call to action by psychiatrist <a href="/wiki/Michael_Liebowitz" title="Michael Liebowitz">Michael Liebowitz</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Clinical_psychologist" title="Clinical psychologist">clinical psychologist</a> <a href="/wiki/Richard_Heimberg" title="Richard Heimberg">Richard Heimberg</a>, there was an increase in attention to and research on the disorder. The DSM-IV gave social phobia the alternative name social anxiety disorder. Research on the psychology and sociology of everyday social anxiety continued. Cognitive Behavioural models and therapies were developed for social anxiety disorder. In the 1990s, <a href="/wiki/Paroxetine" title="Paroxetine">paroxetine</a> became the first prescription drug in the U.S. approved to treat social anxiety disorder, with others following.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Social_anxiety_disorder&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
